### [1] [Agentic Analysis for Agentic Infrastructure: An LLM-Powered Pipeline for Comparative Governance of DAO and Corporate AI Protocols](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26203)
*Yutian Wang,Luyao Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LLM-powered pipeline analyzes governance discourse of AI agent protocols ERC-8004 (permissionless) and Google A2A (corporate), finding comparable participation inequality and fragmentation, but denser thematic alignment in permissionless governance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Governance structures shaping AI agent interoperability standards are empirically underexamined despite proliferation of protocols, creating a need for large-scale analysis of socio-technical power structures in technology governance.

Method: Developed LLM-powered comparative pipeline integrating automated annotation, neural topic modeling, and multi-layer network analysis; applied to 4,323 governance participation records from ERC-8004 and Google A2A using LLM-assisted coding and network analysis.

Result: Governance form influences thematic priorities (permissionless vs corporate-led focus), but both regimes show comparable participation inequality and community fragmentation; permissionless setting exhibits denser discourse alignment suggesting open governance fosters greater thematic convergence.

Conclusion: LLM-assisted methods can advance empirical study of technology governance, with findings suggesting open governance may foster thematic convergence; implications for designing more equitable agentic AI standards with data and code openly available.

Abstract: As AI agent protocols proliferate, the governance structures shaping their interoperability standards remain empirically underexamined. We introduce an LLM-powered comparative pipeline for large-scale governance discourse analysis, integrating automated annotation, neural topic modeling, and multi-layer network analysis to study socio-technical power structures at scale. We validate it on two contrasting standards for agent interoperability: ERC-8004 (permissionless, on-chain) and Google A2A (corporate-led). Analyzing 4,323 governance participation records, we combine LLM-assisted coding, topic modeling, and multi-layer network analysis to examine how institutional design shapes thematic priorities and community structure. We find that while governance form influences substantive focus, both regimes exhibit comparable levels of participation inequality and community fragmentation. Discourse alignment is denser in the permissionless setting, suggesting that open governance may foster greater thematic convergence despite decentralized participation. These findings illustrate how LLM-assisted methods can advance the empirical study of technology governance, with implications for designing more equitable agentic AI standards. All data and code are openly available.

</details>


### [2] [Instruction Bleed: Cross-Module Interference in Prompt-Composed Agentic Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26356)
*Ching-Yu Lin,Yifan Liu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces Compositional Behavioral Leakage (CBL), a phenomenon where editing one prompt module in an agentic system affects other modules despite no direct dependencies. The authors develop a protocol to measure CBL and demonstrate its existence in a deployed job-evaluation agent.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Practitioners observe that when editing prompts in agentic systems, changes to one module can silently shift the behavior of other modules even without shared variables or dependencies. This interference problem lacks formal study and measurement protocols.

Method: The authors formalize CBL and develop a three-channel protocol (volume, content, and form perturbations) to probe it on a deployed job-evaluation agent using Claude Sonnet 4.6 with 144 trials. The protocol perturbs non-focal modules while measuring effects.

Result: Only the content channel produced a detectable paired effect (Cohen's d = 0.63), while no recommendations flipped completely. This reveals a sub-threshold regime invisible to standard QA but impactful across thousands of decisions in deployed agents.

Conclusion: CBL represents a new failure axis orthogonal to known agent problems. The paper establishes cross-module interference measurement as essential for prompt-composed agent evaluation, providing an operational definition, reusable protocol, falsifiable predictions, and system characterization.

Abstract: Practitioners of prompt-composed agentic systems report a recurring failure mode: editing one prompt module silently shifts the behavior of others despite no shared variable or executable dependency. We formalize this as compositional behavioral leakage (CBL): interference between modules sharing a context window. CBL is enabled by architectural non-isolation: transformer self-attention provides no formal boundary between concatenated modules. We probe CBL on a deployed job-evaluation agent (Claude Sonnet 4.6, 144 trials) through a reusable three-channel protocol that perturbs non-focal modules along volume, content, and form. Only the content channel produces a detectable paired effect (Cohen's d = 0.63, bootstrap 95% CI excluding zero); no recommendation flipped -- a sub-threshold regime invisible to standard QA but compounding across the thousands of decisions a deployed agent makes. CBL is orthogonal to known agent-failure axes (adversarial injection, cognitive degradation, multi-agent fault propagation, privacy leakage). We contribute an operational definition, a reusable protocol, a falsifiable prediction set, and a system-class characterization, establishing cross-module interference measurement as a requirement for prompt-composed agent evaluation.

</details>


### [3] [Detecting and Controlling Sycophancy with Cascading Linear Features](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26155)
*Maty Bohacek,Rishub Jain,Nicholas Dufour,Thomas Leung,Chris Bregler,Roma Patel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A novel iterative data generation pipeline that isolates cascading linear features for better interpretability and control of model behaviors, demonstrated on sycophancy in language models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current activation steering methods require clear contrastive sample pairs to interpret and control model behaviors, but these binary pairs limit feature disentanglement and interpretability reliability.

Method: Developed an iterative data generation pipeline that moves beyond binary pairs to isolate samples showing degrees of features that scale linearly with behavior, enabling better feature disentanglement.

Result: Sycophancy features discovered through cascading samples form linearly separable subspaces, allowing better selection of model activations corresponding to desired behavior; outperform LLM-as-a-judge and system prompting baselines with lower computational demand and better interpretability guarantees.

Conclusion: Cascading linear feature isolation enables more effective detection, deterministic scoring, and robust steering of model behaviors like sycophancy while improving interpretability and computational efficiency compared to existing methods.

Abstract: Interpreting and controlling model behaviors through activation steering methods requires many pairs of contrastive samples that clearly exhibit desired or undesired behavior. These data pairs determine the degree to which interpretability frameworks can reliably detect model features responsible for a behavior, and therefore the ability to steer models toward or away from such behavior. In this work, we present an iterative data generation pipeline that isolates cascading linear features responsible for a behavior. Specifically, we show how moving beyond simple binary pairs of samples, and instead isolating samples that show degrees of features that scale linearly with behavior, allows for better disentanglement of features. We focus on detecting and steering away from sycophancy -- the tendency of language models to prioritize user validation. We demonstrate that sycophancy features discovered through cascading samples form linearly separable subspaces, and allow for selection of model activations that more clearly correspond to the desired behavior than baseline approaches. We also evaluate their ability to enable detection, deterministic scoring, and robust steering, and see that they either match or outperform LLM-as-a-judge and system prompting baselines while providing lower computational demand and more interpretability guarantees. Code & Data: https://cascading-feats.github.io/

</details>


### [4] [Life After Benchmark Saturation: A Case Study of CORE-Bench](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26158)
*Nitya Nadgir,Sayash Kapoor,Kangheng Liu,Peter Kirgis,Matilda Orona,Stephan Rabanser,Tilman Bayer,Abhishek Shetty,Yue Ling,Derrick Chan-Sew,Rumi Nakagawa,Saiteja Utpala,Zachary S. Siegel,Arvind Narayanan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper argues that benchmarks shouldn't be retired just because accuracy saturates, proposing a multi-dimensional evaluation framework including construct validity, generalizability, efficiency, reliability, model vs. scaffold importance, and human-agent collaboration uplift.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Benchmarks are typically retired when accuracy saturates, missing opportunities to study other important dimensions of agent performance beyond just accuracy.

Method: Uses CORE-Bench Hard (computational reproducibility benchmark) as a case study to measure agent performance across six dimensions: construct validity, OOD generalizability, efficiency, reliability, model vs. scaffold importance, and human-agent collaboration.

Result: Identified construct validity threats, created improved benchmark (CORE-Bench v1.1) and OOD task suite. Found CORE-Bench v1.1 remains useful for measuring efficiency, reliability, model/scaffold performance despite accuracy saturation. Human-agent collaboration showed 2x speedup in computational reproducibility tasks.

Conclusion: Provides a more rigorous, multi-dimensional alternative to accuracy-centric evaluation paradigms, demonstrating that benchmarks can yield meaningful insights beyond accuracy saturation.

Abstract: When a benchmark's accuracy saturates, it is often retired and replaced with a more challenging version. We show that this approach privileges accuracy and misses the opportunity to study six other key dimensions of agent performance: construct validity issues such as shortcuts, out-of-distribution generalizability, efficiency, reliability, the relative importance of the model versus the scaffold, and uplift from human-agent collaboration. We use CORE-Bench Hard, a benchmark for computational reproducibility of scientific code, as a case study to demonstrate that measuring agents along these dimensions yields meaningful insights into agent performance even after accuracy saturates. First, we surface threats to construct validity in CORE-Bench Hard that are difficult to anticipate with less capable agents. We introduce an improved benchmark, CORE-Bench v1.1, and an out-of-distribution task suite, CORE-Bench OOD. Second, we find that despite accuracy saturation, CORE-Bench v1.1 remains useful for measuring efficiency, reliability, model performance, and scaffold performance. Finally, we conduct a small-scale randomized experiment to measure uplift from human-agent collaboration on real-world computational reproducibility tasks. We find a statistically significant speedup by about a factor of two -- likely underestimated due to one-fifth of human-only reproductions reaching the time limit before completing -- and describe various other findings. Together, our contributions present a more rigorous alternative to the dominant accuracy-centric evaluation paradigm.

</details>


### [5] [Scientific discovery as meta-optimization: a combinatorial optimization case study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26728)
*Yuan-Hang Zhang,Chesson Sipling,Massimiliano Di Ventra*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Meta-optimization framework for scientific discovery where the evaluation objective itself is optimized via consensus aggregation of LLM-generated criteria, applied to algorithm discovery for 3-SAT problems.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scientific discovery is an optimization problem with vast state space of theories/experiments. While LLMs enable automated exploration, there's need to simultaneously modify evaluation criteria since optimization objective itself should be optimized as understanding evolves.

Method: Proposes formalizing research as meta-optimization with consensus objective aggregation. LLM-generated objective functions are combined via correlation-weighted voting, creating stable, self-correcting evaluation criteria that evolves with deepening understanding.

Result: Applied framework to algorithm discovery for 3-SAT problems using digital MemComputing machines. Reduced baseline scaling from ~N²·⁵¹ to ~N¹·³³, achieving ~67× speedup on largest instances tested.

Conclusion: The problem-agnostic meta-optimization framework with consensus objective aggregation can considerably aid scientific discovery by allowing evaluation criteria to evolve as understanding deepens.

Abstract: Scientific discovery is fundamentally an optimization problem, defined by a vast "state space" of theories and experiments, and an evaluation criterion based on quality, novelty, and validity. Large language models (LLMs) have enabled automated exploration of this space, but we argue that simultaneous modification of the evaluation criteria is equally important. Here, we propose formalizing research as meta-optimization, where the optimization objective itself is also being optimized. Our key contribution is "consensus objective aggregation," where LLM-generated objective functions are combined via correlation-weighted voting, yielding a stable, self-correcting evaluation criterion that evolves as understanding deepens. We apply this framework to algorithm discovery for 3-SAT problems based on digital MemComputing machines, reducing the baseline scaling with problem size $N$ from $\sim N^{2.51}$ to $\sim N^{1.33}$ and delivering a $\sim 67\times$ speedup on the largest instances tested. As a problem-agnostic framework, we hope this approach will considerably aid scientific discovery.

</details>


### [6] [Refusal Lives Downstream of Persona in Chat Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26161)
*Viola Zhong,Qirui Li*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Compliant persona directions in LLMs gate refusal behavior by suppressing it at late layers, requiring persona projection to restore refusal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior work studied refusal and persona directions separately, but their interaction remained unexplored despite both being linear directions in activation space.

Method: Extracted compliant persona and refusal directions from Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct, performed interventions on both directions, and tested projection effects.

Result: Compliant persona steering strongly suppresses refusal (97% → 2% refusal rate in Llama). Refusal direction restoration works only at late layers, not early ones. Projecting out persona direction in late-layer window restores refusal baseline.

Conclusion: Refusal is gated at late-layer expression stage, downstream of computation, and depends on persona, contradicting the view of refusal as an isolated direction.

Abstract: Linear directions in activation space have been identified for both refusal and persona traits in instruction-tuned chat models, but the two have been studied as separate mechanisms. We show they interact: a compliant persona gates refusal. In Qwen2.5-7B-Instruct and Llama-3.1-8B-Instruct, we extract a compliant model-persona direction and a refusal direction and intervene on both. Compliant persona steering suppresses refusal -- in Llama, the refusal rate falls from 97% to 2%. Reintroducing the refusal direction partially restores refusal at late layers but not at early ones. Projecting out the persona direction in a late-layer window restores it to baseline; projecting out a random direction does not. Refusal is therefore gated at the late-layer expression stage, downstream of where it is computed. Treating refusal as a single isolated direction misses its dependence on persona.

</details>


### [7] [Semantic Early-Stopping for Iterative LLM Agent Loops](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.27009)
*Sahil Shrivastava*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes semantic early-stopping for multi-agent LLM loops, replacing fixed iteration caps with metrics based on semantic stability and quality improvement. It provides theoretical foundations, an efficient evaluation protocol, and empirical validation showing significant token savings without quality loss on HotpotQA.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Fixed iteration caps (max_iterations) in multi-agent LLM loops waste tokens on easy inputs while prematurely truncating hard ones, as they don't adapt to whether answers are still improving. The authors seek a smarter, semantic-based stopping criterion.

Method: 1) Semantic early-stopping: halts loops when consecutive draft embeddings stop changing in meaning (cosine distance with patience window) AND answer quality stops improving.
2) Theoretical framework: proves deterministic termination and well-definedness, treating convergence of distance sequence as empirical conjecture rather than overclaimed Banach contraction.
3) Efficient evaluation protocol: generates each question's full trajectory once, replays all stopping policies over identical drafts, and caches LLM-judge calls for strictly paired efficiency-quality comparison.
4) Distinguishes operational tokens (charged to policy) from evaluation tokens (measurement instrument).

Result: Empirical study on HotpotQA (60-question test split):
- Judge-free semantic stopper reduces operational tokens by 38% relative to max_iterations at parity quality (Δ-IS = -0.004, p = 0.81).
- Full quality-gated variant is counter-productive due to per-round judging cost dominating savings.
- Oracle selecting best round attains +0.115 Information Score over all practical policies (p ~ 4e-11).
- Reveals open problem shift: from "when to stop" (easy) to "which round is best" (hard).

Conclusion: Semantic early-stopping effectively reduces token waste in multi-agent LLM loops while maintaining answer quality. The theoretical and evaluation frameworks establish rigorous foundations for studying loop termination. Most importantly, the findings reframe the core problem: instead of optimizing when to stop (which is relatively solvable), the real challenge is identifying which iteration contains the best answer.

Abstract: Multi-agent large language model (LLM) loops, for example a Writer that drafts and a Critic that revises, are almost always terminated by a fixed iteration cap (max_iterations). This is a syntactic kill-switch: it is blind to whether the answer is still improving, so it over-spends tokens on easy inputs and truncates hard ones. We study semantic early-stopping: the loop halts when consecutive draft embeddings stop changing in meaning (cosine distance with a patience window) and the answer's measured quality stops improving. Our work makes three contributions. First, an honest theoretical footing: we prove deterministic termination and well-definedness and machine-check these claims, while treating the convergence of the distance sequence as an empirically tested conjecture rather than a (previously over-claimed) Banach contraction. Second, a judge-efficient evaluation protocol: we generate each question's full trajectory once, replay every stopping policy over the identical drafts, and cache every LLM-judge call, yielding a strictly paired efficiency-versus-quality comparison at low cost; we further separate operational tokens (charged to a policy) from evaluation tokens (a measurement instrument). Third, an empirical study on multi-hop retrieval-augmented question answering (HotpotQA). On the 60-question test split, a judge-free semantic stopper reduces operational tokens by 38% relative to max_iterations at parity quality (Delta-IS = -0.004, p = 0.81), whereas the full quality-gated variant is counter-productive because its per-round judging dominates cost. An oracle that selects the best round attains +0.115 Information Score over every practical policy (p ~ 4e-11), reframing the problem from "when to stop" (easy) to "which round is best" (open).

</details>


### [8] [AlgoEvolve: LLM-driven Meta-evolution of Algorithmic Trading Programs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26173)
*Dhruv Sharma,Gautam Shroff*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AlgoEvolve: LLM-driven evolutionary framework generates and improves trading strategies in Python through iterative evolution and meta-evolution of prompts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Extend LLM-based semantic evolution from static coding benchmarks to algorithmic trading, a domain that is noisy, non-stationary, and highly discontinuous.

Method: LLM-driven evolutionary framework with inner loop generating/improving Python trading strategies and meta-evolutionary outer loop evolving prompts guiding program synthesis.

Result: System exhibits emergent regime-adaptive strategy logic and autonomous shifts in trading rules; meta-evolution discovers improved search heuristics that outperform human-designed instructions.

Conclusion: LLM-based semantic evolution provides viable approach for continual program synthesis in complex environments like algorithmic trading.

Abstract: Recent work shows that Large Language Models (LLMs) can act as semantic mutation operators for the evolutionary discovery of programs and proofs. Most current applications focus on static coding benchmarks. We extend this paradigm to algorithmic trading. This domain is uniquely challenging because it is noisy, non-stationary, and highly discontinuous. We present AlgoEvolve, an LLM-driven evolutionary framework that generates, evaluates, and iteratively improves executable trading strategies. These strategies are expressed as Python code and evaluated through a rigorous testing protocol. Across multiple experiments, the system exhibits emergent regime-adaptive strategy logic, including autonomous shifts in trading rules. We further introduce a meta-evolutionary outer loop that evolves the prompts guiding program synthesis in the inner loop. This outer loop discovers improved search heuristics. These heuristics balance exploration and exploitation while reducing zero-trade failures. They consistently outperform initial human-designed instructions. The results demonstrate that LLM-based semantic evolution provides a viable approach for continual program synthesis in complex environments.

</details>


### [9] [Knowledge-augmented Agentic AI for Mental Health Medication Information Seeking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26205)
*Huizi Yu,Jian Liu,Wenkong Wang,Lingyao Li,Jiayan Zhou,Zhaoqian Xue,Xiang Li,Xinxin Lin,Zhiying Liang,Zhuoru Wu,Siyuan Ma,Xin Ma,Lizhou Fan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A multi-agent framework integrating patient narratives and regulatory data for psychiatric drug safety, using knowledge graphs to preserve source provenance


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Patients seek medication info online, but psychiatric drug safety knowledge is split between authoritative regulatory records and experiential patient narratives, risking poor contextualization that can amplify fear and non-adherence

Method: Developed a provenance-aware, knowledge-graph-based multi-agent framework unifying Reddit posts, WebMD reviews, and FDA Adverse Event Reporting System records for nine antidepressants, using LLM entity recognition benchmarked against physician annotations

Result: Patient communities were far more concordant with each other than with regulatory reports, forming independent safety signals; for sertraline, adverse events appeared in community sources hundreds of days before FDA dates

Conclusion: Source-aware integration enables more auditable psychiatric medication information, with provenance preservation keeping claims traceable and regulatory facts distinct from patient experience

Abstract: Patients increasingly seek medication information online, yet safety knowledge for psychiatric drugs is split between regulatory adverse-event records, which are authoritative but abstract, and patient narratives, which are experience-near but unvalidated. Integrating them without conflating evidence and anecdote is especially consequential in psychiatry, where poorly contextualised information can amplify fear, nocebo responses, and non-adherence. Here we develop a provenance-aware, knowledge-graph-based multi-agent framework unifying 466,525 Reddit posts, 60,782 WebMD reviews, and twenty years of U.S. FDA Adverse Event Reporting System records for nine antidepressants. A large-language-model entity-recognition pipeline benchmarked against physician annotations reached highest F1 scores of 0.969 for medications and 0.973 for conditions. The two community platforms were far more concordant with each other (overlap up to a Jaccard similarity of 0.905) than with regulatory reports, indicating that patient-generated data form a partly independent safety signal. For sertraline, many adverse events appeared in community sources hundreds of days before the corresponding FDA date. A Neo4j knowledge graph grounded in ATC-N, ICD-10, and MedDRA vocabularies preserves provenance, keeping every claim traceable and regulatory facts distinct from patient experience. These results establish source-aware integration as a route to more auditable psychiatric medication information, with usefulness and patient benefit to be tested prospectively.

</details>


### [10] [Accelerating Skill Assessment in Chess: A Drift-Diffusion-Enhanced Elo Rating System](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26267)
*Tianyuan Zhou,Zhizheng Fu,Tianming Yang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A new chess rating system called DD-Elo uses move-by-move gameplay data to detect skill changes faster than traditional Elo, while maintaining theoretical alignment with Elo and being backward-compatible.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional Elo rating systems suffer from response lag because they only use match outcomes and ignore the granular quality of gameplay. Incorporating move-level information is challenging due to noise and vast game-state space.

Method: The authors propose the Drift-Diffusion-Enhanced Elo Rating System (DD-Elo), inspired by the drift diffusion model from cognitive neuroscience. By modeling skill expression as a decision-making process, DD-Elo integrates move-level data to capture rapid skill fluctuations while proving mathematically that it maintains bounded deviation from traditional Elo.

Result: Extensive experiments show that DD-Elo adapts to skill changes faster than Elo while maintaining theoretical alignment with the traditional system. The model offers an explainable, highly responsive, and backward-compatible solution for chess rating ecosystems.

Conclusion: DD-Elo provides a novel approach to skill assessment that addresses the limitations of traditional Elo systems by incorporating granular gameplay data while maintaining compatibility and theoretical soundness. The implementation is publicly available for broader adoption.

Abstract: Rating systems such as Elo serve as the gold standard for matchmaking in competitive chess. However, they inherently suffer from response lag due to their exclusive reliance on match outcomes, neglecting the granular quality of gameplay. Nevertheless, incorporating move-by-move information into rating adjustments presents a significant challenge given the substantial noise and the vastness of the game-state space. To address this, we propose the Drift-Diffusion-Enhanced Elo Rating System (DD-Elo), a novel skill assessment framework inspired by the drift diffusion model (DDM) from cognitive neuroscience. By modeling skill expression as a decision-making process, our model integrates move-level data to capture rapid skill fluctuations. We provide a rigorous mathematical derivation proving that DD-Elo maintains a bounded deviation from the traditional Elo system, ensuring theoretical alignment. Extensive experiments demonstrate that DD-Elo adapts to skill changes faster than Elo. Our findings suggest that DD-Elo offers an explainable, highly responsive, and backward-compatible solution for chess rating ecosystems. The implementation code is publicly available at https://github.com/Aquila-zhou1/DD-Elo .

</details>


### [11] [Governing Actions, Not Agents: Institutional Attestation as a Governance Model for Autonomous AI Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26298)
*Jakob Salfeld-Nebgen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AI agents need governance for high-risk actions; this paper proposes requiring independently attested evidence at point of action, separating reasoning from execution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As autonomous AI agents begin performing irreversible consequential actions (like clinical prescribing and software deployment), there's a need for governance models to ensure safety and accountability.

Method: Formalize a computational governance model where agents retain autonomy over planning/reasoning but lack execution authority for high-risk actions. Execution requires preconditions independently attested by separate authoritative sources, cryptographically bound to declared intent, and evaluated by deterministic policy. Decisions are logged tamper-evidently.

Result: Proof-of-concept implementation created and illustrated with examples from software deployment and clinical prescribing domains.

Conclusion: This governance model offers a practical approach to AI safety by adapting proven institutional patterns to AI systems, enabling accountability while preserving agent autonomy.

Abstract: Autonomous AI agents may begin to perform consequential, irreversible actions such as clinical prescribing and production software deployment. This paper observes that human institutions have governed powerful autonomous actors not by monitoring their reasoning but by requiring independently attested evidence at the point of consequential action. We formalise this institutional pattern as a computational governance model for AI agent systems. Under the proposed model, an agent retains full autonomy over planning and reasoning but holds no execution authority over designated high-risk actions. Execution is conditional on preconditions that are each independently attested by a separate authoritative source, cryptographically bound to a declared intent, and evaluated by a deterministic policy. Decisions are recorded in a tamper-evident log amenable to independent re-verification. We present a proof-of-concept implementation and illustrate the model with examples from software deployment and clinical prescribing.

</details>


### [12] [COrigami: An AI Pipeline for Co-Designing Flat-Foldable Visually Recognisable Origami](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26299)
*Tom Zahavy,Shaobo Hou,Thomas Tumiel,James Doran,Francesco Faccio,Xidong Feng,Alex Havrilla,Igor Khytryi,Chenglei Li,Lisa Schut,Vivek Veeriah,Arijan Abrashi,Michał Kosmulski,Robert J. Lang,Nick Robinson,Brandon Wong,Marcus Chiam,Gloria Fang,Satinder Singh*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: COrigami: An AI pipeline that generates origami crease patterns from natural language while satisfying both geometric constraints and aesthetic evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current generative AI struggles with physical art creation that must satisfy both strict geometric constraints (like flat foldability in origami) and subjective visual aesthetics.

Method: End-to-end pipeline: 1) Generate semantic stick figure from natural language, 2) Compute base packing, 3) Solve for flat-foldable crease pattern, 4) Shape the pattern, 5) Refine using RL-driven autonomous aesthetic evaluation loop.

Result: COrigami acts as a collaborative assistant that generates structural starting points for human artists, satisfying multi-objective physical constraints and enabling mathematically grounded co-creativity.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates how AI can integrate algorithmic optimization with autonomous aesthetic critique to enable reliable, mathematically grounded co-creativity in domains requiring both physical constraints and visual aesthetics.

Abstract: While generative AI has achieved remarkable success in solving problems with verifiable solutions, generating physical art that satisfies both strict geometric constraints and subjective visual aesthetics remains a challenge. This paper presents an approach to tackle these difficulties in the domain of computational origami, a mathematically rigid environment that grounds artistic design within the equations of flat foldability. We present COrigami, an end-to-end AI-driven pipeline that assists the design cycle by generating crease patterns from natural language. Our pipeline involves generating a semantic stick figure, computing a base packing, solving for a flat-foldable crease pattern, shaping the flat-folded crease pattern, and refining the generated model using reinforcement learning driven by an autonomous aesthetic evaluation loop. Our system acts as a highly effective collaborative assistant, generating structural starting points that human artists can further expand and shape. By integrating algorithmic optimisation with autonomous aesthetic critique, this work demonstrates how AI systems can satisfy multi-objective physical constraints to enable reliable, mathematically grounded co-creativity.

</details>


### [13] [The Verification Horizon: No Silver Bullet for Coding Agent Rewards](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26300)
*Binghai Wang,Chenlong Zhang,Dayiheng Liu,Jiajun Zhang,Jiawei Chen,Mouxiang Chen,Rongyao Fang,Siyuan Zhang,Xuwu Wang,Yuheng Jing,Zeyao Ma,Zeyu Cui*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper examines the "verification gap" where verifying solutions has become harder than generating them for AI coding agents, proposes a framework for evaluating verification signals, and shows that co-evolving verification with generators is essential as AI capabilities improve.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The traditional intuition that verification is easier than solution generation is being inverted for modern AI coding agents. While foundation models can now generate complex solutions, reliably verifying these solutions against human intent has become the primary challenge due to the inherent underspecification of intent and the optimization gap that widens during training.

Method: The paper characterizes verification signal quality along three dimensions (scalability, faithfulness, and robustness) and studies four different reward constructions: (1) test verifier for general coding tasks, (2) rubric verifier for frontend tasks, (3) user as verifier for real-world agent tasks, and (4) automated agent verifier for long-horizon tasks. It conducts extensive experiments across different task types and policy capability levels to analyze reward design challenges and effective use of reward signals.

Result: Experiments demonstrate that targeted verification design can effectively suppress reward hacking, improve task completion quality, and achieve significant performance gains across multiple internal and public benchmarks. The results show that no fixed reward function remains effective as policy capability grows.

Conclusion: Verification must co-evolve with the solution generator; it cannot remain static. As AI policy capabilities continue to improve, verification mechanisms need to adapt accordingly to maintain effectiveness in ensuring solutions meet human intent.

Abstract: A classical intuition holds that verifying a solution is easier than producing one. For today's coding agents, this intuition is being inverted: as foundation models develop stronger reasoning capabilities and engineering harnesses grow more sophisticated, generating complex candidate solutions is no longer difficult -- reliably verifying them has become the harder problem. Every verifier we can build is only a proxy for human intent, never the intent itself. This makes verification subject to a twofold difficulty: first, intent is underspecified by nature, making it inherently hard to faithfully check whether it has been fulfilled; second, during model training, optimization widens the gap between proxy and intent -- manifesting as reward hacking or signal saturation. To address this, we characterize the quality of verification signals along three dimensions -- scalability, faithfulness, and robustness -- and argue that achieving all three simultaneously is the central challenge. We further study four reward constructions: a test verifier for general coding tasks, a rubric verifier for frontend tasks, the user as verifier for real-world agent tasks, and an automated agent verifier for long-horizon tasks. Across different task types and policy capability levels, we conduct in-depth analysis and experiments on the core challenges of reward design and how to more effectively leverage reward signals. Experiments show that targeted verification design can effectively suppress reward hacking, improve task completion quality, and achieve significant gains across multiple internal and public benchmarks. These experiences collectively point to a core observation: no fixed reward function can remain effective as policy capability continues to grow; and verification must co-evolve with the generator.

</details>

### [17] [Accelerating Returns and the Qualitative Engine for Science](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26359)

This paper gives a simple mathematical interpretation of that claim and then argues that, even if such acceleration is real, it does not by itself resolve the central problem of scientific discovery. This paper positions the Qualitative Engine for Science (QES) [3] as a response to that missing capacity. In this view, the Kurzweil theory helps explain why quantitative capability may accelerate, while QES addresses the central problem in scientific discovery that acceleration alone does not solve. Its value does not depend on when AGI arrives, but on the fact that the processes of scientific discovery themselves constitute a form of human wisdom worth preserving, organizing, and making accessible.

### [18] [Narration-of-Thought: Inference-Time Scaffolding for Defeasible Ethical Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26366)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard chain-of-thought reasoning on moral dilemmas suffers from two critical failures: stakeholder collapse (focusing on only one party) and uncertainty suppression (not acknowledging unknowns before making decisions), which undermines reliable ethical reasoning.

Method: Introduced Narration-of-Thought (NoT), a structured prompting approach that organizes reasoning into five sections: protagonist, stakeholders, two-step consequences, uncertainty, and commitment. This requires no training, parameters, or fine-tuning. Extended to include textual-gradient descent optimization and cross-family training judges, plus a five-round multi-stakeholder debate protocol.

Result: On 100 DailyDilemmas scenarios across four generators, NoT reduced stakeholder collapse from up to 31% to under 1% and uncertainty suppression from up to 72% to 1-24%. Achieved 95% full consensus in multi-stakeholder debates and 100% combined convergence. Showed significant improvements in stakeholder count (Cliff's delta +0.79 to +0.90) and uncertainty scores (+0.65 to +0.93).

Conclusion: Narration-of-Thought provides an effective, training-free solution to critical flaws in moral reasoning by externalizing stakeholders, consequences, and uncertainties, creating an auditable substrate for dependable agent deployment in ethical decision-making scenarios.

Abstract: Standard chain-of-thought on moral dilemmas exhibits two failure modes: stakeholder collapse (the trace names at most one party with a stake in the outcome) and uncertainty suppression (no explicit unknowns or hedges before committing to an action). We introduce narration-of-thought (NoT), a system prompt that structures chain-of-thought into five sections: protagonist, stakeholders, two-step consequences, uncertainty, then commitment. NoT adds no training, parameters, or fine-tuning. On 100 DailyDilemmas scenarios across four generators from three vendors, NoT cuts stakeholder collapse from up to 31% to under 1% and uncertainty suppression from up to 72% to 1-24% on every model. A matched-budget verbose-CoT control rules out token spend as the active ingredient; NoT retains Cliff's delta advantages of +0.79 to +0.90 on stakeholder count and +0.65 to +0.93 on uncertainty score for three of four generators, and a section ablation attributes each shift to its specific sub-instruction. Textual-gradient descent initialised at NoT improves the scaffold further; a cross-family training judge (different vendor from the generator) dominates an in-family one on every measured axis. Extended to a five-round multi-stakeholder debate protocol, the scaffold converts a 6% standoff into 95% full consensus on a calibration set and 100% combined convergence on a DailyDilemmas replication. The resulting traces externalise the stakeholders, consequences, and uncertainty grounding each commitment, providing an auditable substrate for dependable agentic deployment.

### [19] [Geometry-Aware MCTS for Extremal Problems in Combinatorial Geometry](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26399)
*Luoning Zhang,Xu Zhuang,Tianhao Wang,Nathan Kaplan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A Geometry-Aware Monte Carlo Tree Search framework that efficiently solves combinatorial geometry problems by enforcing geometric constraints incrementally and exploiting symmetries, achieving new best-known results for several problems including Max-N3IL.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Classical exact solvers suffer from combinatorial explosion for combinatorial geometry problems, and standard RL/transformer models struggle with sparse reward "validity cliff" and quadratic token consumption limits when dealing with geometric constraints in grid configurations.

Method: Proposes Geometry-Aware Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) framework that strictly enforces geometric constraints through incremental updates to feasible action space, reducing constraint checking complexity from O(n³) to O(n²) for collinear constraints. Uses geometric symmetries in two ways: canonical pruning during node expansion to reduce branching factor, and symmetric batch transitions to accelerate discovery of promising configurations.

Result: Established new best-known computational results on 5 out of 6 considered problems. For Max-N3IL, found configurations of size ~1.8n for grids of size 82 ≤ n ≤ 119. For Smallest Complete Set problem, found configurations of size ~0.95n, providing new upper bounds within tested grids.

Conclusion: Geometry-Aware MCTS is established as a highly adaptable framework for discovering novel configurations in combinatorial geometry, effectively overcoming the limitations of classical solvers and standard ML approaches through incremental constraint enforcement and symmetry exploitation.

Abstract: We study certain extremal problems in combinatorial geometry that ask about configurations of points in an $n \times n$ grid that satisfy strict, global geometric constraints. Classical exact solvers suffer from combinatorial explosion for these types of problems, and standard reinforcement learning and transformer-based models struggle with the sparse reward "validity cliff" and quadratic token-consumption limits. To overcome these bottlenecks, we propose a Geometry-Aware Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) framework. Our approach strictly enforces geometric constraints through incremental updates to the feasible action space. For constraints about collections of collinear points, like those that occur in the classic No-Three-in-Line problem (Max-N3IL), this mechanism reduces the constraint checking complexity from $O(n^3)$ to $O(n^2)$. To improve search efficiency, we exploit geometric symmetries in two ways: canonical pruning during node expansion to reduce the branching factor, and symmetric batch transitions to accelerate the discovery of promising configurations. We perform extensive experiments and establish new best-known computational results on five out of six of the problems that we considered. Notably, for Max-N3IL we find configurations of size roughly $1.8 n$ for grids of size $82 \le n \le 119$. For the Smallest Complete Set problem, we find configurations of size roughly $0.95 n$, providing new upper bounds within the tested grids. This work establishes Geometry-Aware MCTS as a highly adaptable framework for discovering novel configurations in combinatorial geometry.

### [21] [Unbiased Canonical Set-Valued Oracles Via Lattice Theory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26418)
*Jobst Heitzig*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper addresses the self-reference problem in oracle AIs by proposing a credal set approach with Knaster-Tarski fixed-point construction to ensure unbiasedness and self-consistency when predictions influence outcomes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Oracle AIs that predict future probabilities face a self-reference problem: their predictions become known and acted upon, changing the very probabilities they were asked to report. Counterfactual approaches (like Scientist AI) become irrelevant once learned because their premise becomes false.

Method: The authors propose having the oracle report a credal set (set of probabilities) rather than a single probability. They use the Knaster-Tarski fixed-point theorem on the complete lattice of closed credal sets, taking the least fixed point of a suitably defined isotone operator to find a canonical, nontrivial self-consistent set. This approach generalizes from binary events to arbitrary random variables.

Result: The construction yields existence, self-consistency, and nonemptiness of the canonical answer. It collapses to classical point answers for non-performative questions. For binary events, under natural hull-factoring assumptions, the canonical answer is an interval. The approach extends lattice-theoretically from binary events to arbitrary random variables.

Conclusion: The paper presents a mathematically rigorous solution to the oracle self-reference problem using credal sets and fixed-point theory, offering advantages over counterfactual approaches by maintaining relevance and self-consistency. The work opens questions about interval characterization in the generalized case.

Abstract: A non-agentic "oracle" AI that estimates probabilities of future events faces a self-reference problem: once its answer is learned and acted upon, it can change the very probability it was asked to report. One response, advocated for the Scientist AI programme, is to ask only counterfactual questions, evaluated as if the answer had no influence. We observe that such answers tend to become irrelevant the moment they are learned, precisely because their premise is then false. We therefore explore a self-referential alternative in which the oracle reports not a single probability but a credal set that is simultaneously unbiased and self-consistent with the consequences of being learned. The naive self-consistency requirement is satisfied by too many sets (including the useless answer $[0,1]$), so the problem is to single out a canonical, nontrivial member. We do so with the Knaster--Tarski fixed-point theorem on the complete lattice of closed credal sets, taking the least fixed point of a suitably defined isotone operator; a variant instead reports the least fixed point that contains every self-consistent point estimate. We prove existence, self-consistency, and nonemptiness, show that the construction collapses to the classical point answer for non-performative questions, and that for a binary event the canonical answer is, under a natural hull-factoring assumption, an interval. The development is purely lattice-theoretic and extends unchanged from a binary event $B$ to an arbitrary random variable $X$, with $P(B\mid A,C)$ replaced by the conditional law $\mathcal{L}(X\mid A,C)$. We close with open questions, including whether the interval characterization itself survives that generalization.

</details>


### [22] [Estimating Uncertainty in Classifier Performance with Applications to Large Language Models and Nested Data](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26422)
*Kylie Anglin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: This paper evaluates confidence interval methods for text classification performance metrics in social science research, addressing common issues like small sample sizes, infrequent constructs, and nested data structures.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Text classification is increasingly used in social science research to measure constructs from natural language, but confidence intervals for performance metrics are inconsistently reported or estimated with inappropriate methods, especially with small datasets, rare constructs, and nested text data within individuals.

Method: The paper conducts simulations to evaluate confidence interval methods under typical social science conditions: small to moderate sample sizes, infrequent constructs, and texts nested within individuals. It compares various methods including Wald interval, basic percentile bootstrap, Agresti-Coull, Wilson, Clopper-Pearson, and proposes a novel pseudo-count regularized bootstrap (especially for F1 score). It also examines hierarchical vs. cluster bootstrap approaches for nested data.

Result: Default methods (Wald interval and basic percentile bootstrap) are least accurate, sometimes with coverage far below nominal 95%. Better methods include Agresti-Coull, Wilson, Clopper-Pearson, and the proposed pseudo-count regularized bootstrap. For nested data, adjustment for effective N and appropriate degrees of freedom is necessary. Hierarchical bootstrap outperforms cluster bootstrap when individuals produce moderate numbers of texts, but is overly conservative with few texts per individual.

Conclusion: The paper provides guidance on appropriate confidence interval methods for text classification in social science, aiming to improve transparency and encourage better sample size planning at the design stage by addressing the specific challenges of small datasets, rare constructs, and nested text structures.

Abstract: Researchers increasingly use text classification--supervised models or large language models--to measure constructs from natural language, providing metrics such as recall and precision as evidence of their validity. Yet, though these metrics are point estimates subject to sampling variation, measures of uncertainty are inconsistently reported alongside them. Further, when they are reported, they are often estimated with methods that are not appropriate when relevant labelled datasets are small or performance is high. To increase and improve confidence interval reporting in the field, this paper evaluates confidence interval methods for performance metrics under conditions typical of social science text classification: small to moderate sample sizes, infrequent constructs, and texts nested within individuals. Across simulations, default methods such as the Wald interval and the basic percentile bootstrap are the least accurate, with coverage sometimes far below the nominal 95% level. Accuracy is improved with the use of Agresti-Coull, Wilson, Clopper-Pearson, and a novel pseudo-count regularized bootstrap (which is particularly relevant to the calculation of F1). When texts are nested within individuals, we demonstrate that adjustment for both effective N and the appropriate degrees of freedom is necessary for producing accurate analytic intervals. Among bootstrap intervals, the hierarchical bootstrap is more accurate than the cluster bootstrap when individuals produce a moderate number of texts but overly conservative when individuals produce only a few. By providing guidance to the field on appropriate interval estimation, we aim to improve the transparency of machine learning applications, and to encourage greater attention to the validation sample size at the design stage.

### [23] [Data-driven Machine Learning Cannot Reach Symbolic-level Logical Reasoning -- The Limit of the Scaling Law](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26454)
*Tiansi Dong,Mateja Jamnik,Pietro Liò*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Supervised deep learning cannot achieve symbolic-level syllogistic reasoning due to fundamental methodological limitations, even with infinite scaling, while symbolic sphere neural networks can do this without training data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To investigate the scaling law limit for logical reasoning: whether data-driven ML systems can achieve symbolic-level syllogistic reasoning (like sphere neural networks do without training data) by simply increasing training data and time.

Method: Theoretical analysis of two methodological limitations in supervised deep learning for syllogistic reasoning, experimental testing of Euler Net's reasoning capabilities, and evaluation of ChatGPT variants (GPT-5-nano and GPT-5) on syllogistic statements with different surface forms.

Result: 1) Training data cannot distinguish all 24 valid syllogism types; 2) End-to-end mapping creates contradictory training targets between pattern recognition and logical reasoning components. Experimental results show Euler Net fails, and ChatGPT reaches 100% accuracy but still provides incorrect explanations, with performance affected by surface forms.

Conclusion: Supervised machine learning systems will not achieve the rigor of symbolic logical reasoning, even with perfect accuracy on training data, due to fundamental architectural limitations that prevent genuine logical reasoning.

Abstract: Sphere neural networks have achieved symbolic level syllogistic reasoning without training data, raising the question of where the limit of the scaling law for logical reasoning lies, i.e., whether data-driven machine learning systems can achieve the same level by increasing training data and training time. We show two methodological limitations that prevent supervised deep learning from reaching the symbolic-level syllogistic reasoning: (1) training data can not distinguish all 24 types of valid syllogistic reasoning; (2) end-to-end mapping from premises to conclusion introduces contradictory training targets between neural components for pattern recognition and logical reasoning. Beside theoretical analysis, we experimentally illustrate that Euler Net cannot achieve rigorous syllogistic reasoning. We further challenge the most recent ChatGPTs (GPT-5-nano and GPT-5) to determine the satisfiability of syllogistic statements in four surface forms (patterns): words, double words, simple symbols, and long random symbols, showing that surface forms affect the reasoning performance and that ChatGPT GPT-5 may reach 100% accuracy but still provide incorrect explanations. As empirical training processes are stopped after achieving 100% accuracy, we conclude that supervised machine learning systems will not attain the rigour of symbolic logical reasoning.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Data collection is a major bottleneck in AI-based scientific automation pipelines. Computational cognitive science is well-positioned for automation because theories are often represented as code, and crowdsourcing enables programmatic human data collection at scale.

Method: The auto-psych system uses nested agent-based discovery loops: inner loop generates, fits, and critiques probabilistic cognitive models; outer loop designs experiments, launches them online via crowdsourced survey experiments, and analyzes the collected data. Tested on classic cognitive psychology case of judging subjective randomness of coin flip sequences.

Result: The system quickly recovers ground-truth theories from synthetic data via systematic experimentation, with nested structure being critical to model performance. In three independent sequences of human experiments, auto-psych finds theories that fit the data better than those generated from scientific literature.

Abstract: AI-based scientific automation is increasingly possible by using agents to generate hypotheses, design experiments, and analyze data. Psychology, and computational cognitive science in particular, is well-positioned to benefit from AI experimentation because theories are often represented as code and crowdsourcing platforms enable programmatic human data collection at scale. Here, we apply automated discovery techniques to the project of generating theories in computational cognitive science, with an agent-based system collecting human data independently through crowdsourced survey experiments. As a testbed, we use a classic case study from cognitive psychology: judging which sequences of coin flips seem subjectively more random. Our system, auto-psych, uses nested agent-based discovery loops to generate explanatory theories of human behavior. The inner loop conjectures, fits, and critiques probabilistic cognitive models; the outer loop designs experiments to test these models, launches them online, and analyzes the data. This system can quickly and reliably recover ground-truth theories from synthetic data via systematic experimentation, but the nested structure is critical to model performance. Further, in three independent sequences of human experiments, the system finds theories that fit the data better than theories generated from the scientific literature. This work thus demonstrates the feasibility of automated data collection and theory discovery in computational cognitive science.

</details>

### [27] [Humans Disengage, Reasoning Models Persist: Separating Difficulty Registration from Deliberation Allocation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26502)
*Han-yu Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Humans and Large Reasoning Models show opposite patterns: humans spend less time on questions they get wrong (giving up), while LRMs spend more tokens on questions they get wrong (uncertainty-driven longer chains).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how deliberation patterns differ between humans and LRMs when solving reasoning problems, moving beyond surface-level similarities in response time across items to examine within-item patterns.

Method: Analyzed a public matched human-LRM corpus with 5 thinking LRMs, separating registration (how response time tracks difficulty across items) from allocation (within-item spending on right vs wrong responses). Used item fixed effects and compared against non-thinking baselines.

Result: Humans and all 5 LRMs show cross-item alignment (registration), but diverge within items (allocation): humans spend less time on items they get wrong (d=-0.41), while LRMs spend more tokens on items they get wrong (Cohen's d=1.47-3.13). This dissociation holds under item fixed effects, replicates across datasets, and is absent in non-thinking baselines.

Conclusion: The human pattern reflects engagement vs abandonment (people persist when expecting to solve), while LRMs show uncertainty-driven chain growth (longer traces when uncertain). Both produce similar cross-item difficulty correlations, explaining why prior work found alignment, but fundamental control policies differ within items.

Abstract: Large reasoning models (LRMs) take longer on harder problems, just as humans do. This surface similarity hides an opposite pattern within items. When an LRM gets a problem wrong, it spends more tokens than when it gets the same problem right; humans do the reverse, spending less time on the trials they get wrong. We separate two levels of deliberation: how response time tracks difficulty across items (registration), and, with item identity held fixed, whether an agent spends more on its own failures or successes (allocation). On a public matched human-LRM corpus, humans and all five thinking LRMs reproduce the known cross-item alignment (registration) but diverge within items (allocation): every LRM shows a large wrong-vs-right effect (Cohen's d = 1.47-3.13 on H-ARC) while humans show the opposite sign. The comparison stays inside each agent's own scale; we never put seconds and tokens on one axis. The dissociation holds under item fixed effects, replicates across datasets, and is absent in a non-thinking baseline. We read the human pattern as engagement versus abandonment: people stay on items they expect to solve and give up on the rest. We read the LRM pattern as length driven by uncertainty: chains grow when the model is unsure, which is exactly when it tends to fail. Both policies produce the same cross-item correlation with difficulty, so they look aligned on the measure prior work has used; the divergence shows up only once item identity is fixed. Under resource-rational metareasoning, the split is between two stopping policies that share a difficulty signal but implement opposite control; trace length captures the signal and misses the control.

</details>

### [30] [Radical AI Interpretability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26523)
*Daniel A. Herrmann,Benjamin A. Levinstein*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A framework for interpreting AI systems as agents using philosophical radical interpretation and mechanistic interpretability to solve for beliefs, desires, and meanings from computational facts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Increasing importance of AI safety requires trustworthy systems through understanding goals or detecting deception. Current interpretability tools lack settled criteria for success in reading beliefs/desires from model internals.

Method: Combines philosophical radical interpretation tradition with mechanistic interpretability tools. Proposes criteria on both representationalist and interpretationist approaches with tests current methods can carry out.

Result: Demonstrates that belief/desire attributions cannot be made piecemeal - they are jointly constrained by propositional structure. This holism is crucial for AI systems that may not share interpreter's concepts but provides analytical leverage.

Conclusion: Provides a needed framework for evaluating interpretability methods by showing how attitudes and propositional structure mutually constrain each other, with mechanistic interpretability enabling measurement of both for AI agent interpretation.

Abstract: We develop a framework for interpreting AI systems as agents, drawing on the philosophical tradition of radical interpretation and the tools of mechanistic interpretability. The core question is: given the computational facts about a system, how do we solve for its beliefs, desires, and meanings? This matters increasingly for safety. We want to be able to trust the systems we deploy, whether by understanding their goals or, more modestly, by reliably detecting deception. Interpretability researchers are building tools to read beliefs and desires off a model's internals, but there is no settled account of when such a tool has succeeded. This book supplies one. We propose criteria on both representationalist and interpretationist approaches, and tie each to tests current interpretability methods can carry out. A central lesson is that these attributions cannot be made piecemeal. Beliefs, desires, and the propositional structure they presuppose are jointly constrained, and a method that fixes one while measuring the others inherits whatever distortions that introduces. This holism becomes pressing for AI systems, which may not share the interpreter's concepts. However, it also provides leverage: a system's attitudes constrain its propositional structure, that structure constrains which attitudes can be attributed, and mechanistic interpretability can help us measure both.

### [31] [PMDformer: Patch-Mean Decoupling Information Transformer for Long-term Forecasting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26549)
*Ao Hu,Liangjian Wen,Jiang Duan,Yong Dai,He Yan,Dongkai Wang,Jun Wang,Yukun Zhang,Ruoxi Jiang,Zenglin Xu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: PMDformer introduces patch-mean decoupling to separate trend and shape information, with Trend Restoration Attention and Proximal Variable Attention to effectively model shape similarity and cross-variable relationships for long-term time series forecasting.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformer-based models using patch-based strategies struggle to accurately model shape similarities across patches and variables due to scale differences, which limits their effectiveness in capturing true shape patterns in long-term forecasting.

Method: Proposes PMDformer with three key components: 1) Patch-mean decoupling (PMD) separates trend and residual shape information by subtracting patch means, 2) Trend Restoration Attention (TRA) reintegrates decoupled trend while computing attention, and 3) Proximal Variable Attention (PVA) focuses cross-variable attention on recent time segments to avoid outdated correlations.

Result: Extensive experiments show PMDformer outperforms existing state-of-the-art methods in both stability and accuracy across multiple long-term time series forecasting benchmarks.

Conclusion: PMDformer effectively addresses scale differences in patch-based Transformers by decoupling trend and shape information, enabling better capture of shape similarities and cross-variable relationships for improved long-term forecasting performance.

Abstract: Long-term time series forecasting (LTSF) plays a crucial role in fields such as energy management, finance, and traffic prediction. Transformer-based models have adopted patch-based strategies to capture long-range dependencies, but accurately modeling shape similarities across patches and variables remains challenging due to scale differences. To address this, we introduce patch-mean decoupling (PMD), which separates the trend and residual shape information by subtracting the mean of each patch, preserving the original structure and ensuring that the attention mechanism captures true shape similarities. Futhermore, to more effectively model long-range dependencies and capture cross-variable relationships, we propose Trend Restoration Attention (TRA) and Proximal Variable Attention (PVA). The former module reintegrates the decoupled trend from PMD while calculating attention output. And the latter focuses cross-variable attention on the most relevant, recent time segments to avoid overfitting on outdated correlations. Combining these components, we propose PMDformer, a model designed to effectively capture shape similarity in long-term forecasting scenarios. Extensive experiments indicate that PMDformer outperforms existing state-of-the-art methods in stability and accuracy across multiple LTSF benchmarks. The code is available at https://github.com/aohu1105/PMDformer.

### [33] [EvoOptiGraph: Weakness-Driven Coevolution via Graph-Based Structural Generation for Optimization Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26578)
*Qingcan Kang,Mingyang Liu,Xiaojin Fu,Shixiong Kai,Tao Zhong,Mingxuan Yuan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: EvoOptiGraph is a framework where data and model co-evolve using MILP graph evolution and weakness-targeted training to improve LLMs on optimization modeling from natural language.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Two main challenges: 1) Training corpora lack structural diversity for optimization modeling, 2) Data generation pipelines are static and decoupled from model learning, preventing targeted improvement based on model weaknesses.

Method: Represents MILPs as attributed bipartite graphs, applies validity-preserving evolutionary operators for structural diversity, converts to solver code/natural language via deterministic compilation and verified back-translation. Uses two-stage training: SFT on initial dataset followed by RLVR (reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards) where graph-derived weakness signals guide new instance generation targeting model failures, creating a continuous feedback loop.

Result: Significantly outperforms larger generalist models, agentic methods, and specialized baselines on six public datasets in accuracy, executability, and generalization.

Conclusion: Targeted data-model coevolution is an effective strategy for improving LLMs on optimization modeling tasks, demonstrating that adaptive feedback loops between model performance and data generation yield substantial performance gains.

Abstract: Automating optimization modeling from natural language with large language models (LLMs) faces two key challenges. First, training corpora lack structural diversity. Second, data generation pipelines remain static and decoupled from model learning. To address these challenges, we propose EvoOptiGraph, a novel framework where data and model co-evolve, driven by model weaknesses. EvoOptiGraph represents each mixed-integer linear program (MILP) as an attributed bipartite graph and applies validity-preserving evolutionary operators to generate structurally diverse instances. The evolved graphs are converted into solver code and natural language via deterministic compilation and verified back-translation. Training proceeds in two stages: supervised fine-tuning (SFT) on an initial dataset, followed by reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR), where graph-derived weakness signals guide the generation of new instances targeting the model's failures. This forms a closed loop that continuously updates the training distribution. Empirical results on six public datasets show that EvoOptiGraph significantly outperforms larger generalist models, agentic methods, and specialized baselines in accuracy, executability, and generalization. These results demonstrate that targeted data-model coevolution is an effective strategy for improving LLMs on optimization modeling tasks.

Prior work has explored workflow reuse and executable skill induction, but it remains unclear which task scenarios admit procedural skills and how the shared procedural structure should be represented across successful traces. We study this problem in FSM-defined scenarios, where successful traces can be viewed as paths in an unknown transition graph, and formulate procedural skills as reusable parameterized control-flow subgraphs. Based on this view, we introduce SkillDisCo, a distillation-and-compilation framework that distills reusable PFSM subgraphs from successful traces and compiles them into callable, executable, and verifiable procedural skills. Motivation: Most existing post-training alignment works withhold detailed data construction, filtering rules, and training recipes, which hinders community reproducibility and lightweight model optimization. Method: Two orthogonal model branches: general instruct model and complex reasoning-specialized model. Curated 3.84M multi-source SFT samples and 200K verifiable RL candidate pool. Designed end-to-end data processing stack with response distillation, multi-dimensional cross-verification filtering, difficulty grading, task classification, and diversity-aware sampling. For Instruct branch: three-stage optimized supervised fine-tuning plus GRPO reinforcement learning. For Reasoning branch: medium-difficulty GRPO RL. Also investigated single-teacher and multi-teacher OPD (MOPD) as RL alternatives.

Result: Instruct branch: SFT improved average benchmark score from 55.01 baseline to 60.99; GRPO RL further elevated to 61.85. Reasoning branch: GRPO RL improved average reasoning score from 73.88 to 75.17. Single-teacher OPD: outperformed RL baseline by 3.26 points on IFEval with +4.43 average overall gain. Multi-teacher OPD: lifted average performance by 4.18 over base model with only 10K samples.

Conclusion: The work provides a fully reproducible empirical post-training recipe for 8B-scale LLMs and comprehensively dissects capability trade-offs among instruction adherence, mathematical reasoning, code generation, and general knowledge, demonstrating significant improvements through transparent, data-driven methods.

Abstract: Post-training alignment determines the reasoning and human preference following capabilities of large language models, yet most existing works withhold detailed data construction, filtering rules and training recipes, which hinders community reproducibility and lightweight model optimization. This work presents NebulaExp, a fully transparent, ablation-driven post-training pipeline built on Qwen3-8B-base, covering two orthogonal model branches: general instruct model and complex reasoning-specialized model. We curate a raw corpus of 3.84M multi-source SFT samples and a 200K verifiable RL candidate pool, and design an end-to-end data processing stack including response distillation, multi-dimensional cross-verification filtering, fine-grained difficulty grading, task classification and diversity-aware sampling. For the Instruct branch, our three-stage optimized supervised fine-tuning approach NebulaExp-Ins-SFT improves the average benchmark score from the 55.01 baseline of Qwen3-8B-nothink to 60.99. GRPO reinforcement learning then further elevates the average score to 61.85. For the Reasoning branch, medium-difficulty GRPO RL improves average reasoning score from 73.88 to 75.17. To address RL's dependency on task verifiers, we systematically investigate single-teacher and multi-teacher OPD (MOPD): utilizing merely 4K instruction-following samples and outperforms RL baseline by 3.26 points on IFEval with +4.43 average overall gain; MOPD fuses four domain-specialist teachers with merely 10K samples, lifting average performance by 4.18 over the base model. This report provides a fully reproducible empirical post-training recipe for 8B-scale LLMs, and comprehensively dissects the capability trade-offs among instruction adherence, mathematical reasoning, code generation and general knowledge.

</details>


### [40] [Do Safety Guardrails Need to Reason? LeanGuard: A Fast and Light Approach for Robust Moderation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26686)
*Dongbin Na*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Safety guardrails don't need chain-of-thought reasoning - a lightweight label-only encoder matches or outperforms reasoning-based guards with 100x less compute.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current guardrail methods use chain-of-thought reasoning for safety screening, making them heavy and slow. This doesn't match practical deployment needs where guardrails should run efficiently on devices like robots. The paper questions whether safety guardrails actually need reasoning at all.

Method: Train a lightweight bidirectional encoder (LeanGuard) and a reasoning guard on the same corpus, then remove only the reasoning while keeping everything else fixed. The 395M parameter label-only encoder uses single forward pass over ≤512 tokens, enabling controlled same-base comparison.

Result: Chain-of-thought does not improve moderation accuracy. LeanGuard achieves average F1 of 82.90±0.26 on public benchmarks, matching reasoning guards built on larger decoders while using ~100x less inference compute. It's more robust to training-label noise and retains better recall at strict false-positive rates.

Conclusion: Current guardrail benchmarks may not be hard enough to reward reasoning, and the necessity of chain-of-thought for moderation remains unproven. Lightweight label-only guards can match or outperform reasoning-based approaches while being far more efficient.

Abstract: In order to screen a prompt or a response, the recent guardrail methods generate a chain-of-thought (CoT) before they issue a verdict. This design follows a common belief that step-by-step reasoning improves a decision. However, CoT also makes the guard heavy and slow, because the model must generate many tokens before it decides. This may not match how guardrails are actually deployed. A guardrail sometimes should not be heavy and slow, and it often runs on-device, for example on an embodied robot. In this paper, we pose a question whether a safety guardrail really needs to reason. To answer this question, we train a lightweight bidirectional encoder and a reasoning guard on the same corpus, and we then remove only the reasoning while we keep everything else fixed. With this controlled same-base comparison, we show that the chain does not improve moderation accuracy. We name the resulting guard LeanGuard. A 395M label-only encoder reaches an average F1 of 82.90 $\pm$ 0.26 over public benchmarks. It matches a reasoning guard that is built on a much larger decoder, while it uses only a single forward pass over an input of at most 512 tokens. This is about a ~100x reduction in inference compute. We further show that this label-only encoder stays robust under training-label noise and retains far more recall at a strict false-positive rate than the reasoning guard, so a heavier reasoning guard is not the more robust choice either. Our finding suggests that the current guardrail benchmarks may not be hard enough to reward reasoning, and that the necessity of CoT for moderation is still not proven. We release all source codes and models including LeanGuard at https://github.com/ndb796/LeanGuard.

LithoDreamer captures feature changes between adjacent states to model stage-specific physics-informed latent spaces, in which it controls process intervention exploration and drives subsequent state transitions. To achieve interpretable intervention optimization without continuous supervision, we propose a contrastive variational optimization paradigm that contrasts the latent differences between intervention paths with variational evolution constraints, guiding the model to generate evolutions consistent with real lithography physics. ### [46] [ResilPhase: Plug-and-Play Phase Mapping and Noise-Resilient Macro-Trajectory Extrapolation for Diffusion Acceleration](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.26769)
